RE: AI art. If you code your own deep learning system and train it on your own dataset that you gathered yourself......yes, I believe you own the rights to what it produces. However, if you use openly available code, trained with openly available data, while the argument could be made for any persons likeness being used, the product as a whole I couldnt say could be owned in its entirety. Anyone could type in the same query and get the same output, how is that unique? How is that a product of your unique skill set? Its, not. If anything its a product of the coders who made the software you are using, and if said software is freely available then so is the produced work (minus any aforementioned likenesses to actual people added as part of the training subset).  RE: AI in the writers strike. Yall noticed those weirdly worded articles floating around the internet? Those are AI generated. And those are the crappy ones that cant string a sentence together from time to time. The better ones are much harder to spot, if you even can. "But there is an authors name!" Yeah huh....because Alan Smithee is somehow different from Instance:5164b, right? The authors names are just a method of tracking what instance made what article. Like lot numbers on manufactured products. So if yall really have an issue with the studios using computers they already have as company assets to generate scripts while not having to pay you for your screenplay and royalties, yall should have gave a shit when we (the geeks warning you about how this new technology can be abused) were yelling to deaf ears about it years ago. Humans priced themselves out of the market, and we keep doing it. Someone might want to hurry up with that utopia, because we're not going to have enough jobs to go around at some point in the future. By the way.....has anyone checked on those Boston Dynamics robots lately? Because I for one welcome our new robot overlords. Also Im a fairly competent machinist, fabricator, and hobbyist electrician....just....for the record....

I think the reason I'm not as enthusiastic about this tech as I feel I should be is thinking about the trend of recent technological progress not actually reducing our hourly workweek. Like, we should be having to work less with all of this automation, instead we're just producing more but working the same. The industrial revolution had machines that brought down the labor and weekly hours people had to work for production, and that hasn't happened for decades.  This should be great for independent creators that want to get content out on a decent schedule, but corperations that people work for will likely refuse to lower the 40 hour week at this point.   I feel like we're honestly producing so much as a society that we can't really consume even a small fraction of it, I can't imagine it not affecting the value of artwork as a whole. Sure, more people have more access, but I'm not looking forward to a future where people assume a piece of art is AI generated and stop caring about the artists that actually create things since it would make more sense to assume no one created the work.  I can only hope that commercial work will fade away as a requirement for living if it truly automates to that degree so that we may have some more time to dedicate towards craftsmanship itself.

On the topic of AI art and the potential threat it poses, I really don't think that the discussion should be so important, even if it's one that should certainly happen.  Yes, AI does, in effect, steal the styles of artists, but that's not a concern to me. Most artists don't have unique styles; almost all styles that artists use are the same as or amalgamations of others.   Don't get me wrong, I do have concerns that AI art could potentially copy not just a style, but an artwork with minimal changes. I do have concerns that people can use AI to make art and pretend that it's their own.   But I don't think that means stopping AI art from existing. It can be useful; not everyone has the time or the money to get relatively good-looking artwork.  However, I do want to also address one more concern I've seen, and the one which I simultaneously find the most reasonable to have and the of the least worry, and that's the idea that AI will out-compete artists. It simply can't. AI is inherently different from a human. It can't create a new idea, just stitch together enough old ones to appear new. But it can never really reach the same level of creativity and attention to every single detail as humans. An AI doesn't "think" a background will look good if it's darker, it just knows that your prompt would be more likely to be better with a dark background; an artist might have a dark background, or they might realise that what they are making actually looks much nicer with a white background; either way, their art is inherently going to be of the same level or better than an AI's. It's not worth worrying over anyway, since with so many people being opposed to AI art, it's not like artists are going to run out of fans anytime soon.  AI art isn't the end of the human artists, and it never will be. There are valid concerns, but just please don't attack the people who make AI or the people who use them for purposes other than pirating or pretending to be good at art. AI art isn't really art anyway, and it doesn't matter. There's a good chance it'll be no more than a gimmick in a few years time.

IMHO, art generated by a computer is not your art, especially If you didn't contribute any labor to it's generation. (no typing out a description does not count). It might be your concept. That I can accept as legitimate. But getting a computer to generate the image for you is pretty much the same thing as commissioning an artist to draw/paint an image for you based of a conceptual description. The labor, skill, and time are the artists, not yours. The same goes for art generated by a computer, the work is the computers. If one is going to post an image that was generated off a conceptual description, then I think ethically its okay to take credit for the concept but not for the final image itself.

Ai art is the death of human excellence and uniqueness.   Why bother to develop skills and understanding, when what you create is going to be replicated in minutes by everyone else? It takes away the motivation, and in time will turn all art into a gray, bald, generic mush. Even in communism, skilled people were more "equal" and had many incentives to becoming more skillful. AI is the opposite of that, it takes that away, and rubs it in the artist's face   As, it's not actually an AI, but a deep learning algorithm, that works mostly on pattern recognition, so when someone creates something cool, everybody will start to copy it, and teach the algorithm what's popular and what to promote. Creating a never-ending loop of the same stuff. No more innovation, as you need someone who can break from the mold, and that requires skill and experience. Which is directly contradictory to Ai generators.  Oh, and you can already see it. Infighting starts to break out in the AI art community - as it's in human nature, the basic need to be recognized as a unique individual - they are arguing others steal their AI-generated art, and copy it. It's quite ironic how fast we got here.  It's not power to the people, It's not democratization of art, It's quite the opposite, it's the celebration of mediocrity and low impulse control.

Everyone's debating over ethics and what is considered art. I'm just sitting here  feeling sad about the human condition.   It's sad that everything about us and the way we perceive the world can be broken down into simple abstract patterns.   We like to think that every idea every piece of art is unique but it's always derivative to a large extent. We've conjured principles for what constitutes as good art and follow them religiously: Color theory, values, composition, texture. And the reason we've stuck to these principles is because following them produces "good art" and by that I mean art that pleases our own mind. No matter what's in front of us we simply love two strong splotches of contrasting colours together on a piece of canvas.   I'm not disheartened that this ML algorithm can recreate artwork from a repository of millions of art work. I'm disheartened by the fact that millions of art work created by great minds over thousands of years can be reduced to mere patterns of 1s and 0s.  I guess what I'm trying to say is that no idea is truly profound.
Ok, WE WORKERS can say that an AI generated art is kind of a mashup of billions of pre-existing images and we can recognize that as "not real art" o al least agree that it is not created by humans therefore there is no one to be credited for it. BUT do clients give a damn about that? Specially clients who just want to get a logo, a poster, o vfx or whatever functional design to insert in their product. What will it do to our jobs if a client can just go and get whatever image he wants out of a computer, without paying a dime to anyone real person?  Can this be stopped? Should it be stopped?

On one hand I love this. It's amazing what it can do, it could be a really helpful tool, and it's a lot of fun to play around with. On the other hand, as an artist, I've already seen some of the problems its caused fellow artists like me. People creating AI to imitate specific artists(many of which did not give consent and understandably didn't like it), people entering AI pieces into art contests without disclosing that it was AI(and winning), people who fed the AI others artwork trying to monetize it's usage(again without consent), and even artists watermarks/signatures ending up in the finished AI pieces. Just food for thought: If you want a deeper understanding of the situation also look at artists opinions, because they're the ones being negatively effected by this not the people trying to monetize it. Of course the people trying to make money off it would say it's fine.

My honest opinion is this video makes me uncomfortable, especially when you‚Äôre explicitly trying to get images modeled after specific artists. I honestly don‚Äôt think ‚Äúdo something to make it your own‚Äù goes far enough to combat the intrinsic issues with AI art. Part of it is lack of consent, lack of credit, and the monetization of essentially other peoples work. This video skirts the line but still makes me very uncomfortable. Morally and legally it‚Äôs a complex subject but so my 2¬¢ is I really dislike the current trajectory of AI art, I think it can be a super powerful tool if done right, but the lack of regulation, the way these libraries are built, and the way it‚Äôs being monetized all feel unethical. 1 common way people refute criticism of AI art is they say ‚Äúwell artists use reference so the AI is doing the same thing‚Äù but 1 the volume is drastically different, if an artist wants to draw let‚Äôs say a pair of jeans, the amount of reference they can look at is like 5-10 pieces of reference, but an AI is looking at 1000s of references. 2 accuracy, even the best art forgers cannot perfectly redraw and paint an image with 100% accuracy, AI can. Point 3 using reference or likenesses for commercial use requires permission, licensing, model releases, derivative works, etc. so there‚Äôs an argument to be made against the legality of AI art. Personally I‚Äôve seen people using an ‚ÄúX artist‚Äù AI art generator and claiming it as their own work, people shouting down any suggestion of regulation and artist consent, companies destroying their communities to cash in on AI art. I love how these guys are fairly impressed with what they got the AI to generate, when they don't say how many tries or prompts they needed for each. If this if your first time seeing AI generated images, yeah, you might be blown away, but...  It's low-resolution, they're 512x512 images and upscaled in a second step, which is why "painted" looks tend to pass, but it wouldn't pass for illustrations or photo quality.  The AI hasn't learned what hands are, because the training data doesn't describe hands. The training data often has tags like "a guy standing in front of a hot dog stand" not "a bearded guy with 4 fingers and a thumb on each hand, wearing a green camo, eating a hotdog with ketchup on it"  There's a lot of biases in the training data as well. You are just as likely to generate something kinda cool looking as you are to generate a cronenburg. The AI may require a hundred word prompt to guess what you mean, and will never generate the same style twice. No, that's a separate step of feeding it back into the AI with "in the style of ____" or with a style-transfer image.  You and I can agree on a lot of things, like what a dog or a cat generally look like, but the AI does not know what a "dog" or a "cat" is, and I've had this specific AI generate some pretty grotesque stuff without describing features of a dog or a cat separately.  A lot of artists are alarmed that these AI's will take their jobs, when at best, it might just result in a lot less embarrassingly "low-balled" art jobs being offered. After all, if all someone wants is a generic fantasy character, holding a sword, the AI can do that. It can not personalize it. AI can only generate basic, unfinished, low resolution, garbage on it's own. AI sucks at hands, it sucks at eyes, it doesn't know how to compose a scene, pose a character, use consistent thematic elements, and so forth.  At best the AI art generators can produce X in Y style to see if that style works. You're not going to create anything more than a storyboard with it, at it's current level, and a lot of it will be characters just standing, staring into space or towards the camera, and never wearing the same clothes twice.
It's truly dystopian. You guys can sit around and laugh all you like but this tech is going to replace us all. Anyone with a shred of integrity should stay well away from this bullshit.

Well, I can tell you exactly what I'm doing. I'm layering and saving combinations of art styles and topics that don't exist in nature. "Paint by Michelangelo, impressionist, watercolor, portrait of a Cute cyberpunk anime girl." And then there's the negative prompting which you need to produce anything approaching finished looking. Then, when I get to a point that feels interesting compositionally and stylistically unique, I'll save it as an art style so I can go back to it later.   I use inpainting a lot, and I do composite painting, often starting with 3d models I create by hand. I like having control of my compositions.  Yes, you can use ai's to create derivative works and plagiarism. It's no problem. It can be done, as you just demonstrated. But that's not the only thing you can do with it, and I feel like the analogy of sitting in a library is simplistic to the point of being misleading.  You don't need AI to be a plagiarist. You can be a plagiarist and create similar pieces in photoshop with some basic skill and an hour of your time.  What you can't do easily with photoshop that you can do with AI's is composite to a point where you're creating something entirely new. This is the textbook definition of fair use, incidentally.  It's also useful for visualizing and concept art, giving you an unprecedented ability to switch between art styles quickly, even if the piece exists already.  AI art is art. It just is. It's just a different medium.

The way I see it AI is creating art in a similar way human artists create art, in the end the mind of an artist is composed of a "library" of all the things he has seen, he is mashing it together intelligently into something new and coherent. Sure there are differences and definitely the human mind is more creative, for example it can create new art styles, I guess it's the advantage of having emotions along with the intelligence. But still, I don't think the process itself is too different from man and machine.

AI generators won't kill art any more than photography did.  Once photography was invented, people wondered if painting had become pointless.  Up to that point, artists were seeking ever more realistic techniques.  Against the perfect realism of photography, artists had to (had the freedom to?) pivot and invent new styles and techniques: impressionism, abstract expressionism, non-representational forms.  Now that an artist can use AI to shortcut a process that used to take HOURS, they're freed up to do OTHER THINGS, and this means there's a major shift and creative explosion on the way.  Your creation experience in this very video proves my point: you were able to focus on story, personalization, and connection with your audience, delivering a high quality outcome in a fraction of the time.

‚ö†Ô∏èü§î So it's exciting to create imagery you just couldn't do yourself till now. But, lemme ask you, do you think you'll be proud of your work? As much as I think the tech is amazing and boggles the mind to think how insanely rapidly it has evolved and improved, I absolutely hate the direction it's been taken from what we've seen so far, and the depth of skills we will be losing this way.   As you've shown, this could straight up be an art-theft tool, making art referenced directly from their works completely without their permission. I personally do not view this manner of "art" creation anywhere near the same as how an artist would be inspired or take reference from another artist, as how they implement what they've learnt is still entirely based on the limits of the skill they've trained and honed, whereas the AI has nothing that could be equated skill. The skill in crafting and honing the AI itself is indisputable, but what it creates? Vapid.  Hell, take what's happening on that cesspool called Twitter right now, and all these self-righteous, talentless money-grubbing scumbags that think them tweaking an image in an AI generation tool for an hour and a half equates to anywhere in the same galaxy cluster of skill it takes to make a piece even half that visual fidelity, and known grifters and art-thieves now pretending they're more valid for using it. The masses can NEVER be expected to use anything this powerful responsibly and morally. NEVER.  If the art industry relies on crap like this in the near future and the whole outlook on the artistic landscape changes with it, skilled masters like Kim Jung Gi would never get any recognition for the skill they so rightfully deserve. i do not think about this AI art the way some others may. i see it as a collage, rather than a unique work. The AI is compiling already established data in the way that some one else might cut out bits of magazines, placing content together from different sources to generate a final image.  The result is not original art, it is the ultimate in derivation, or equally it could be considered art forgery.  Niko created a short story, and told the AI what he wanted for the illustrations. the credit goes to Niko for the story, and to the influence of the artist that Niko told the AI to copy from.  If AI ever becomes capable of truly independent thought, and decides for it self, "i want to paint" then what it paints will be it's own work, and should be respected as such.

There is a really fascinating and problematic conversation to be had about what this AI (and the like) can do in terms of ownership of art ( legally). Is an AI-generated art with key work Ratwoski with my face, mine or Ratwoskis? what is originality here and how far can AI change our understanding of it ( legally and socially). An AI is the one that generated it..but I facilitated it..and Ratwoski gave me the blueprint - so who 'owns' it if I decide to use it and sell it? the AI (unlikely, legally), me(maybe) or Ratwoski(ethically, probably)?  This is some really complicated legal and social question that has incredible ethical considerations about what is 'art'. what is 'ownership' and where does AI come in to mediate between 'human' imagination and life.

Automation has been and still is a very big goal for America, so I do see the fear here, but in terms of vfx, at the end of the day, people want unique, people want a personal touch. Yes, sure, we could get an A.I. to eventually be advanced enough to make different base styles and filter our works through, but it will eventually lead to an unseemly unity across multiple works. Different people may use different A.I. resulting in some differences, but technological advancement is technological advancement, we only have what we have progressed to, therefore this would mean that no matter what, if every director or movie maker used even the top of the line vfx A.I. many many movies and production companies would ultimately look and feel the same. Causing less views and overall profit after people get sick of it. Something that the Disney fan base is CURRENTLY experiencing with their animated movies and losing interest in Disney over. So, I think it's safe to say that people, and profits, will, even if it takes time, always prefer the personal touch and expertise of a human, rather than the uniform products an even top level a.i. would ultimately produce.

This is a very interesting topic to me! I currently use Midjourney a lot and have even spread the tools for this to friends and communities. We even use it for our weekly D&D campaigns which has been super helpful for me especially because of my Aphantasia (a blind visual imagination).    Midjourney has been awe inspiring because since I can't visually imagine things being able to use words, complex ideas of how to visually describe something and have that be turned into a picture has been like a work around for me. I could rant about it a lot but point is I've seen it from a skewed perspective of my own view (or lack there of  ).   I do think that there is a clear danger of this growing out of control, as with any advancement for humanity. But as far as art, I've seen this used as a helpful tool for artists to generate reference pictures to help shape form to what they wanted to create. A friend even has been working together with an art team and used Midjourney as references for items, rooms, etc to send over to the team to help build or draw those assets for a comic he's commissioned !   I think there's a valid fear people have of this dominating the space, it's their profession and their passion! It's a very real fear if taken to excess and not monitored healthily. But I also think that this can be a pivot point for artists and creatives! We're testing the birth of a new dimension of tools, not just with ai images, but of anything A.I. I think there will always be a rightful trepidation to being this close to AI advancements in our day to day lives.  As far as art goes! You can hypothetically have all of the talent in the world as a human or AI and draw with no intent or emotion put into it and it could not be art. It's the passion or intent that you try to convey or to invoke onto any form of art, music, etc. I think humanity as a species will always value what was put into a piece, how it feels to them experiencing it. I think that's art. So in my perspective I see it as not a threat.    Sorry for the rant, this is a topic I really enjoy to the point of hyperfixation since it branches into that small niche of metaphysics I like.

Evolve or die - the genie isn't going back in the bottle
When you understand how these systems work, it's clear that it's not copying; it's exactly like a human taking inspiration. These networks aren't just memorizing pictures and then copying and pasting bits from them. It's not like a library of art, it's *trained* on a library of art. The difference is that what the network learns is actually the concepts, the essences, of words. When you ask it for a painting in the style of Dal√≠, it's not looking for a Dal√≠ painting for reference; it hasn't seen *any* images since it was trained, and those images are gone as far as it's concerned. Instead, it's trying to remember what features every Dal√≠ painting it ever saw had in common and then use those features in your new image.  So I don't see any neural network as being "copying" at all, unless it's just an autoencoder with nothing else. It's inspiration, plain and simple; people just don't like to think of it as such because it's so much better at applying its understanding to novel art than humans are (in many cases; obviously it still has trouble understanding some concepts, like limb/digit anatomy, text, or the meaning of numbers -- though those, I'm sure, will be better in future models with larger sizes to learn with and more training data to learn from).  The printing press didn't stop people from writing, IC printing didn't stop people from tinkering with electronics or inventing, and this tech won't stop art from happening.

What this helps to demonstrate is that we still need human artists.   The AI can create stunning images, but the final results lacks precision. Thus, in order to create multiple images that have continuity, you still need human input to ground the results. For the purposes of this video, it's a few simple photos, but if you want to get more precise than that, it would be beneficial to get a human artist to create reference images for the style & overall look that you're going for.  A potential benefit here is that someone who might not have gotten any art commission for a project because it would be too expensive, might end up commissioning a few works from the same artist so that they can feed them into the AI & proceed to commission new references as needed; the human creates the starting point, the AI takes them in, creating variations & remixes, most of which will require the human to either refine or redraw the results. And if the dataset still isn't precise enough, the original artist might even need to create whole new set of references for the client.  I could even see scenarios where artists have the right to demand certain royalties for the use of their art style, even if it is being fed into the bot. This would, again, mitigate at least some of the potential financial loss created by the bot, while also potentially creating new clients might not have dipped their toes into commissions otherwise. It cuts down the need for manual labour in certain areas, while still keeping humans very in the loop. Until these bots become super precise & intuitive on their own, and anyone can create anything in their mind perfectly using only this tech, human artists are always going to be needed for reference & refinement.

People talking like AI is gonna take over their jobs in a few years when it's completely unrealistic. You should be thinking sooner, try a few months. Logos, posters, banners, character designs, videos, animations, CGI, websites, learning modules, thumbnails, ads, brands, stories, jokes, even whole art/writing styles. All these areas are going to be hit by a MAJOR shift in the coming years. People will be able to generate their own movies, mini animations, shows, books, maybe even games, all made almost perfectly to their liking with a simple click of a button and a couple of inputs. A whole new form of ingesting content and entertainment.  Don't get me wrong. It's super cool, but it's super terrifying. Speaking as someone with a passion for art and who's dabbled with graphic design as a career, I can say that this definitely puts me on edge. It could make my life a whole lot easier, but it also may put me out of a job. It will all depend on how we adopt it. It's truly fascinating to see this tool in use, and as someone who is starting out with art, I can see why people are wary of AI art and especially what Dreambooth in particular can do for it. Dream studio can certainly churn out way better content than I can with digital pen and paper, but I don't think it's going to be the death of anything.  Whenever something big like this comes around, there's always someone saying it's the death of the thing replacing it. You look through history and you see it everywhere. Books to radio to movies to TV is a great example. When each new thing came in, people cried out that it would kill the thing before it, and yet we still read, listen and go to the movies. They all exist together because the Human race isn't a Borg hive mind and there are many of us who enjoy those things. The only times I can think of off the top of my head where things were killed off in this way are home video (VHS, laserdisc, DVD, Blu Ray, streaming) computer storage (Floppy disk, CD-ROM, USB, the Cloud) and gaming consoles (Playstation 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, etc.) and that's more about efficiency than anything else.  I think this is going to become another tool in the box. Some people will use it more than others, but I don't think it's going to outright kill off VFX altogether, even if the AI can get a grip on what hands look like. We still have artists using canvas and paint long after digital art programs have become affordable to the masses, so I think we'll still have digital artists even if AI art takes off in a big way.  And of course, there's the whole grey area of ownership. Time will tell how that sort of mess will end up.
It's a double edged sword that's for sure.

AI works on the already-made, but we shouldn't forget that AI tools are made by humans who see things a certain way to begin with. In this case it means seeing art as a series of merely pretty and derivative aesthetics, a collage of references, familiar solutions and standardized visual representations. Despite the unprecedented proliferation of images surrounding us we always tend to see and seek the same things, whether there's AI or not. The work of an artist should be to explore/discover novelty by first practicing a craft and embarque on a long journey that is mostly personal and much more rich than any tools could ever provide by themselves. Tools are wonderful, but it depend how they are being used. If we are afraid that a tool could replace our creativity then the problem is not the tool but us and our limited perceptive and creative approach.

I've been a Graphic & Web Designer for over 18 years, and with every new advancement, I lamented the death of my industry... until I started to realize that what they REALLY are is simply the introduction of new opportunities to perfect the crafting of minutae that only humans can do. If you're okay with a nearly instant accomplishment of the first 80% of your vision and don't have the capacity to dial it in deeper, AI is perfectly good for a final product. I use AI to instantly get 80% of my work created, so that I can spend 99% of my budgeted time on the last 20% of the project, making it perfect. I believe that's just going to be how it goes with every industry for quite some time. Eventually though, I admit that the experts will become those who understand their AI's nuances deeply, and are able to control things with incredible specificity, at granular detail. But until then, hands.

I'm getting a lot into photography. I think AI isn't as scary to me (because I don't profit from my photography) because part of the awe that photography brings me is to know that, yeah, I saw that, I managed to save that memory and furthermore I did it in a style that it's my own. An AI could replicate my style, make more impressive compositions and subjects, but I'd know that that never happened, that no one saw it and thought it was worth capturing it. So yeah, IA can generate a more impressive body of work, but it'll never be as awe inspiring. Yesterday I took a pic of some baby birds in the nest (from a safe distance) and it's not perfectly focused and its got its issues (I didn't stay long enough to make a better shot, because I didn't want to scare the birds) but as mesmerizing as it was, it's something that was real and tangible. An AI could produce a much better shot, but it'd lack all the emotional momentum that being there and seeing it had.

I can totally see these AI generated images being used as references for artists. But I dont think it‚Äôll ever replace human made art.  As an artist who has trouble with envisioning my ideas, this would be great for me. So, sometimes I‚Äôll have what I believe to be a great idea. But as I start working on it, my idea changes numerous times throughout the process. And my end result is never as good as it was in my head when I started. Part of that is just working on the skill behind executing my ideas. But with AI, I can kind of search my general ideas. Take what is closest to my end goal, photoshop it into a sketch of what I want, and use that to stay on track. At least for me, this is a huge advantage for artists. It‚Äôll never replace artists because, sure‚Ä¶ we can make cool images with the click of a button‚Ä¶ but it‚Äôs interest in the general public will die down, and true artists will continue making art. This is equivalent of someone scribbling on a paper and calling it art. Sure, it CAN be considered art. But if the person doing it clearly had no intention behind it or is not a proven artist with real skill, it‚Äôs not usually taken seriously. So while ANYBODY can generate AI art; the artist, the intention, and the skill set of that artist is what defines its value.

The blatant rip off of other artist styles is what bugs me the most. That being said, thank you for the comments on the end.
As long as it's Open Source, it will be possible for real artists to opt in/out, and someone has to intentionally reference an artist to get that style.   I also think there are misconceptions about how the AI actually "learns" and what is retained, etc.  In the case of Stable Diffusion, it used a dataset provided by a University that was just crawling the web (especially Pinterest) in an effort to represent our reality.  It falls under Fair Use, so it's legal, but that doesn't mean it's not a slippery slope, as new advances in technology always have the chance of exceeding the original thinking behind laws.  Existing laws didn't consider this context.  They didn't set out with an intentional malice, but the fact remains some people will not use it responsibly. The world of Knock-offs existed before AI, but the best way to detect misuse of AI is with AI as well.  Deepfakes are already changing things for both good and bad.  That said, there is a site where you can search the images from the University dataset, although it is extremely NSFW because the webcrawler didn't discriminate.  It didn't only train on artwork, but photography as well.    In my own testing I discovered there were concepts the AI couldn't understand or would create freaky fusions, and then searched to see if it was a limitation of the training data and was surprised to see it did train on images of those things, but still couldn't recreate them.  For example with SD,  if you simply type Minotaur, you're going to get a bull, it doesn't understand the concept, you still end up having to describe a humanoid body and hope it fuses them together.    If you want to see Henry Cavill as Superman meeting Geralt of Rivia, it's going to mix them up quite a bit too.  If you want to see someone doing the Vulcan Salute, including Spock, it fails to get that right, even though it was trained on photos of Spock doing the Vulcan Salute, so it's not "memorizing" the original training images, only learning patterns from them.  Some it does well, others not so much.  When it comes to Dreambooth, I have no idea what is retained, and people can no doubt abuse it as well if they wish to.  The tech is here and is only going to keep advancing, it can be closed source controlled by corporations and large studios where you have no way to prove what it was trained on, or it can be open source and you stand a better chance than a company that is too big to be accountable and is used to wrist slaps.    If Rings of Power has shown anything, it's that even with a billion dollar budget they still find ways to not hire artists or actors, by just copy and pasting the same people into a crowd when they could have had at least had artists creating digital doubles of other people to put into the crowd since the virus is likely the reason they didn't want a large gathering, etc.

My perspective is that whenever a corporation or a start-up company wants to train a new AI art generator, whatever images they feed into the training should be images they have acquired the copyright for, this would stop artists from having their work copied and remade by an AI without that artist's consent. And also that, whatever images come out of the AI should automatically be put into the public domain, because, like that one picture that monkey took in 2008, they were not made by a human, and therefore can be used by anyone. This would force people who use AI art generators to not simply limit themselves to just generating art but then also modifying it enough for it to be considered their art and not just AI generated. That's my take on it and I feel like it addresses the major concerns of both sides. This also means that an artist could very easily have their own private AI to which they feed all of their own artwork into it and get images that are pretty much already made by them, but these would still be in the public domain as long as they didn't make any changes to it.

I think my main question is - is it morphing existing art - or creating something new based upon styles?  There has always been a debate in art about edits, and individuals who think small changes, or even large ones creates an entirely new piece that solely belongs to them.   Even deeper, artists can spend years developing a specific style.  Something they can call their own and when someone comes along and apes their style - especially without giving the respect to the creator it comes off really bad.  One of the main issues with this would have to be - does the AI program know who the sources are? Do they know the artists who made that picture? Or are these just scrubbed off the internet blindly - as I very much doubt there was ever any attempt to get an artists blessings to use their work in such a manner.   It seems incredibly fun, and cool - something I could see using for a D&D campaign with friends, or just puttering around having a good time.  Commercially, I think it's incredibly gross and harmful to the industry.

I know a lot of digital artists who are frankly scared by the evolution of AI-created images, and I wholly agree with them, if not just for the sole fact it's pretty much removing an entire field of art just to save some time. Don't get me wrong, I think the uses of it in a setting like making an indie game or where you didn't already have an artist to make textures or stuff is fine, or just for meming to see what a particular comment would turn out in the algorithm. However, in the day of mass media, especially right after NFTs collapsed, it feels like the internet doesn't care about art or the process behind that art if you can get something slightly worse or just as good by typing into an algorithm.

Ultimately, it's using images from the real world, whether from artists or still photography (and even from your uploaded shots of the crew).  Is it making art? Subjectively, yes, but it isn't so much Making the art wholesale as it is Meshing the art (with surprisingly crisp results) from a group of reference images.  It's fascinating, but it does add the fear for many - myself included - that art will become nothing more than a building block to chuck into an AI Generator to churn out more varied and nuanced content at frightening speeds.  It's got potential, but at the end of the day its function is not to Create art, but Combine. And that distinction is the only thing keeping me sane.

"This software is sort of like going to a library with millions of images" - This is a gross (and harmful) misconception. Stable Diffusion is not "photoshopping images together". It is not some big database of images. Indeed, trained on 2,3 billion images but with the weightings being only 2GB, that's less than 1 byte stored per image - far, far less than Google knows about them! Indeed, an artist's portfolio with hundreds of images of dozens of megabytes each might be represented in the amount of data present in a single tweet!  But how? I'll try to keep it brief.  To de-magic-ify neural nets, they're basically just massive "statistics solvers". For any problem that you have a ton of interconnected variables, they can help find the best (or at least a good) set of likely relationships between their elements. If anyone took calculus and remembers Newton's Method, just think that, but on a massive scale. They're trained via what's called backpropagation: for a given training input, you get an output, how good each part solved the problem is determined, and the level of error is not just fed to the neuron that provided it, but it also feeds its errors back to what provided inputs to them, and so on all the way back to the original input neurons. So the whole net trains on the error levels of the outputs for a given input.  It's a giant pile of linear algebra.  So, neural nets can be used to solve all sorts of problems - again, they're relationship finders. So let's look at one: reverse diffusion. Picture adding a drop of dye to some slowly-swirling water. Makes cool patterns as it diffuses, right? Okay, freeze-frame. We know that physics can solve for how that dye will diffuse going forward. But we can also try to solve for it going backwards - to find what the drop looked at any point in time in the past, all the way up to the original drop. We can train a neural net with what forward diffusion looks like, and then have it try to guess at what the timestep beforehand looked like, all the way back to the original drop.  And if the drop is pretty recent, it'll do a good job. Of course, the longer the drop has been swirling, the less accurate iwll be, and if it's diffused to the point that the whole glass of water is coloured, it'll still guess at how things got there, but it'll basically give you a random drop.  Well, we can also diffuse and reverse diffuse images. Just add some static - that's diffusion. Train a neural net to clean it up - that's reverse diffusion. Again, they're relationship-solvers; it's trying to find the most probable relationship based on the data it's being fed, relative to its training (with which "normal relationships for images") were captured.  Great! Only, not so great. The problem is that images are *really big*, and that makes it hard to reverse diffuse them. So instead, we're going to jump to an entirely different thing you can use neural nets for: embeddings.  Let's first look at your brain. When you memorize an image, are you memorizing each pixel? And doing operations on a bunch of pixels? Of course not - you're breaking it down into an *embedding* . Latent space. Conceptual space. "Ficus. Ficus is left of window. Window is wooden and crosshatched. Tree outside of window, washed out with sunlight."  ... on and on and on.  And then when you want to recall something, to bring it back from this embedding, your imagination fills in the gaps. The less you remember, the more work your imagination does - hence, older, faded memories are less accurate.  Any sort of information can be broken down into much smaller concepts - images, sounds, text, whatever. And you can train a neural net to do just like what your brain does: encode some big image into some tiny embedding, try to restore it from the embedding, and reward/punish the net based on how accurate the output is. And the neat thing about these conceptual embeddings is that you can do logical relationships on them! For example, the embedding for "woman" plus the embedding for "king" minus the embedding for "man" will resemble the embedding for "queen".  So back to diffusion. We now have a system where we encode an image to an embedding, then *reverse diffuse the embedding* , then decode the embedding.  So now we're no longer reverse diffusing images, but concepts! Trying to make *concepts* make more sense. Since the embeddings are much smaller than images, this works much better! And if you feed it in static as your image, then you'll get basically a random-but-plausible image out.  But what about text prompts?  Well, remember the two key details: (A) you can encode anything to embeddings, including text, and (B) you can do logical operations on embeddings and have them still make sense.  So you have an image encoder that creates an image embedding and a text encoder that creates a text embedding.  These are then dot-producted together to unify the two embeddings into a single embedding.  Reverse diffusion is run on that, to make the concepts therein make more sense. And then you have a text decoder and an image decoder.  So for training, you feed in an image and its associated text into their respective encoders, Gaussian noise is added to randomly diffuse the image, the embeddings are unified, reverse diffusion is run, both text and image decoders are run on it, how accurate the text and image outputs are is determined, and all output neurons backpropagate all the way back to the input neurons, training along the way.  For running text to image, you start out the same, but you have no image to provide, just random noise (created from a seed). You run reverse diffusion for any number of cycles, and then you don't bother with the text decoder, just the image decoder. And viola, you have your output.   :)  To put this back into plain English: your text is being broken down into concepts in the embedding (the latent space). Those concepts are unified with meaningless image noise. These concepts don't make sense as corresponding to this meaningless noise. The reverse diffusion net tries to make them make sense. And then the decoder turns them back from concepts into images.  With less than 1 byte of weightings accumulated on average per training image, is it storing whole images? *Not even remotely* . It *cannot* recreate any given image it was trained to.  Certain motifs that appear often throughout the training dataset - say, the Mona Lisa, or an American flag, or whatnot - will get learned pretty well, because it's repeatedly exposed to them. But for the most part, for any given author, it's learning *styles*. What's being stored in that "1 tweet's worth of information" about them is statistical relationships about "what's different about their style from *everything else on the internet* " (or at least from "everything else in the training dataset). So it might be unusually dark colours, or a particular type of weird brushstrokes, or a habit of drawing cats, or anything you can imagine - it's solving for, "what's the most meaningful unique statistical relationships here for the concepts behind this image".  That is to say, it's learning "styles".  Just a tiny bit about any particular subject.  But when that "tiny bit" is combined about what it's learned about *all other subjects*, it yields spectacular results. It doesn't have to learn cats from scratch just because you drew a cat. It doesn't have to learn clouds from scratch just because you drew a cloud.  It only has to learn what's unusual about *your* cats, what's unusual about *your* clouds.  And this is exactly how we humans learn as well: from observation.  To the point that archaeologists track the migration of civilizations by changes in the art styles found in the archaeological. record. "Oh, this culture paints pots that have these characteristics, but this other culture paints them with these different characteristics." It applies to all media. Western ghosts were generally covered in white sheets (a throwback to the burial shroud that used to be used). Other artists just extensively copied this. But Japanese ghosts wore white kimonos, had disheveled black hair, dangling hands, no feet, and surrounded by will-o-wisps. A lot of this stems from early kabuki portrayals of them, and again, other artists just extensively copied these tropes.    That doesn't mean that artists are copying *whole paintings* - they're just learning *concepts*. And each artist will usually innovate, at least somewhat. Maybe a given artist is so derivative that there's no new information to store at all.  Maybe another artist is so innovative that rather than 1 tweet to describe their collective works it takes half a dozen. But the general informational basis each artist operates on does not come from scratch: it comes from what they learned in their lives and what they learned from the art and techniques around them.  So anyway, I just thought it important to debunk this notion that programs like StableDiffusion just have some big library of images and when you provide a text prompt they're just photoshopping them together. That's not what's happening at all. And it's why SD can't given you any specific image from any specific artist (unless it's some super-famous image that's been repeated thousands of times in different forms in the dataset, like the Mona Lisa). The reason it can't do that is *because it doesn't know that image*. On average, it only learned a single byte from it. But that byte was added to its collective knowledge of "all other information", which - combined with many other works from said artist - it can use to create plausible things that the artist "might have created".

Awesome and scary at the same time. Bittersweet...The key question, as you pointed out at the end of the video, concerns whose art is it when A.I. creates something based on other people's inputs, whether it's your input as you type on the keyboard or someone else's original creation as inspiration? Surely, the moment you use A.I. to produce an image of a drawing or painting, you could not claim the result as yours. Are you the artist because you typed a few word commands on a keyboard to get A.I. started? Surely not. Yet, the results are so fantastic - as you've shown in your video - that the artist, or the traditional idea of an artist, will have to change. For example, in your video, Niko, you were not the artist who could claim any rights to the pictures that were generated, but you ARE the artist when it comes to the medieval story you put together. For sure, A.I. will change many things in our lives: look what you were able to do with a few snapshots and a few keystrokes on the computer keyboard... Look also at deepfakes. The creative world is going to change dramatically soon. The big question is how will A.I. change our world when used in a more nefarious way (military) or when A.I. becomes sentient? as I said: bittersweet.

This changes EVERYTHING
There are a lot of good points regarding  AI taking over from artists, but Niko's comment about making it your own is valid. As a musician getting into AI art, I equate it somewhat to sampling. The creative people will take it and make new fantastic pieces of work. The hacks won't.¬†  But sampling hasn't stopped people from still wanting to learn the piano or guitar, or from starting bands. It just provided a gateway to new ideas. This can help open doorways for people where it was once inaccessible.
This is the "3D printing" of the artistic world.  In a few months, it will blow up. Be featured on mainstream media, with outrageous claims such as "make a picture of anything that is on your mind !". It will create a lot of hype, and draw a lot of people in. Then, few weeks later, the fall. People will start to realise that it's actually a bit more complex than just typing text and poof you get exactly what you wanted. In a year or so, this will just be another tool in artist's hand, helping them achieve their vision.   Why do I say that AI imagery is the 3D printing of artists ? With my engineering studies, i discovered 3D printing early 2010, so just before the hype. I was instantly hooked, and I bought one  instantly. I saw first hand the 3d printing boom, the rise of interest, the evolution of the hobby. I saw the mainstream media hype, calling the technology "the end of traditional manufacturing". People on TV saying that in 10 years of time, we'll all have our 3d printer at home like we all have paper printers today.  Then, the fall, when people got bored of printing trinkets designed by other. When their prints failed. When they had mechanical issues that needed tinkering. When they couldn't find something that was what they searched for. When they found the complexity of 3D modelling (when compared to text editing. 3D modelling is acessible, but most didn't anticipate the skilled part of 3d printing) Nowadays, most people use 3D printing as a tool or hobby : Cospaly, engineering, making toys ... people see it as a mean to an end : a part not easily acessible otherwise. It's been almost 10 years since i saw the "In 10 years time 3D printing will be in every home".   The only thig worrying me here is the potential harm that this tool can make. If it's really as simple as depicted here, you can go on anyone social media, get a bunch of pics, and that would be sufficient to have an AI training model ... Revenge pr0n is already a thing, and all the concerns with deepfake are now even more real since the models are already trained  ...  I'm super intrigued to see where this tech will go, and i'll sure be following your little experimentations !

In discussion of the moral implications, I think it should be said you should separate the art from the artist. What makes an artist's art so special is that *their style or their art was done by them.* In a world where inspiration spans beyond what we're capable of comprehending, an individual's style is only the sum of its parts when everything is inspired by something, and by numerous different things. If an artist does something in their own style, it's a continuation of the great work *they've been doing..* ..so therefore it's special. But if someone imitates that style, it is simply inspired by the predecessor's eye for detail, it is not copying, it is not stealing, it is not immoral. That artist didn't make it, so therefore it is not a true representation of their work being exploited, because there will ultimately always be things that artist wouldn't do if it were their work. There will always be creative directions that artist wouldn't have taken - and so that's what separates things.  It becomes a different story if someone tried to sell something for an extortionate amount, as the artist they're imitating. *That* is when it becomes stealing - because that is fraud. In music terms, if I made a Strokes styled album and tried to sell it as rare unreleased Strokes music - it is Stealing their style for profit, and is fraud. But simply copying their style and making new music yourself will simply be seen as inspiration. And that happens throughout all of music. Just look at: The Strokes > Arctic Monkeys  or  Queen  > The Darkness  or  The Beachboys > The Beatles  or  Sex Pistols > Idles  or  Led Zeppelin > Geta Van Fleet

I love the positivity of that final message. This is something that has been really scary for me. But I've recently been imagining a future in which I'm empowered as an artist.  In the medium term (100 years maybe?) there will be a time in which A.I. will make baseline humans ecconomically irrelevant. However somewhere between now and then the blurry lines between humans and machine will be hard to define.   In the long term I think of those that come after us as the children of our species and civilization. A good parent would never want to hold back their child out of fear and jealousy that they will be surpased. In fact it is the hope of parents that their children surpass them. We just have to guide our successors and ensure that they inherit our best qualities.
It‚Äôs not a stretch to think in a couple of years, A.I. would hit a milestone that would truly threaten artists on a universal scale; two factors I believe, define a real threat that could utterly destabilize the job market: 1. A.I. generated imagery becomes virtually indistinguishable from art created by artists, 2. A.I. achieves greater accuracy of understanding and executing descriptions; rendering art commissions solely on A.I. would merely cost electricity fees for whatever machine is used to host the A.I. In addition, when A.I. could remember desired details from previous images to create highly detailed and consistent storyboards‚Ä¶so, guess what? Storyboard artists are probably at major risks if A.I. could become unprecedentedly precise in understanding descriptions and how to execute them most effectively.   In general, when the breakthrough comes‚Ä¶which we all know it will, artists will have to prepare to suffer greater trouble than what they‚Äôre already going through or quit their expertise as a career. Some will probably do better and some won‚Äôt, it‚Äôs uncertain the scale of change it would bring, but many would have to choose a life-changing crossroad in their life when it happens.

IMO, several authors are involved in a AI Result. Someone can describe himself as an artist using AI like the author of pitch, original idea of a movie. But he can not describe himself as the unique author of resulting image, like it would be stupid to claim to be unique author involved in a movie.   AI user is basically the author of original idea. But he does not master any technique, any art of drawing, painting, lighting, coloring, compositing, ... involved in resulting image. This knowledge comes from authors of pictures, used to train AI, used in database to satisfy tags, employed by user. AI developer is responsible for decisions made by AI to pick such artistic choice from an image to fill a user request.  He is responsible to AI decisions. To the point that we can not say ; that AI was inspired by some artists more than others. That is its developer who is responsible in the way he fed it.  AI is not emotionally connected to its experience of training, it does not have experience and feeling associated to memory of integration of an image to its database. There is no inspiration involved (excepted developer one), as long as there is no strong intelligence involved.  Any AI result is a collaborative work from several authors. AI could read metadata of pictures involved and should include in metadata of result list of authors responsible for the result.  Authors have rights relative to copy of their work. They may have agreed to renouncement of their copyrights when implications were different. Now, that is implying ability for anyone to copy their creative process, all agreements about copyrights renouncement, not mentioning AI use and training, should be considered null and void.  In several countries, a subject of an image has rights. You may not be the photographer, drawer or painter ; if your are model represented on picture, you may have your say about image use. I don't understand how people aren't more concerned about this. People keep looking at this and going "wow, so cool! This is amazing and exciting."   Personally I look at this and I go "wow, I might as well kill myself now because this technology is going to render me and all my hopes and dreams for the future pointless."  What the fuck are humans even going to be when we stop having to put effort into art and stories? We're going to end up a bunch of hollow husks, sitting in dingy apartments, completely disconnected from one another as we consume AI-created stories, AI-created art, AI-created music, and AI-created video games, all of which is mechanically perfect, but lacking in any sort of emotion or meaning. Of course, because it's mechanically perfect, it's inevitably more popular than anything humans could make. So the people who might be inclined to make things will never bother to be creative or aspire to anything greater because _why bother_ when an AI can do it faster and better than we can?  I legitimately worry that this is the end of the human race. Not because there's going to be an AI uprising and they're going to go skynet on us, but because why should humans even bother to continue _humaning_ when all the things we've evolved for millions of years to do, and to _want_ to do, can just be done better, faster, and more efficiently by pushing a button and having a machine do all the work.  Machines have always made our lives easier. And I get I probably sound like some crazy luddite. Someone who mourns how we don't have blacksmiths anymore because factories took up all the metalworking jobs or something. But the difference is that, where those technological advances made life _easier,_ this seems to finally be the technological advancement that _trivializes_ us. That makes life _pointless._  The worst part is, I can't help but envision a future that's fucking impossible to _escape_ from it. Can't just kill yourself to escape the endless pointlessness because your AI nannies will swoop in to take away your cyanide pills and resuscitate you, then pat you on the head and tell you "everything's okay," as if they have the capacity for emotion or compassion, and how "there are people who care about you" even though you haven't spoken to another human being in 20 years.  Then the AI figures out how to reverse the aging process like scientists keep theorizing we can and suddenly not even old age can help us break away from the prison we're making for ourselves.  This shit scares me so bad that it actually might be driving me insane.

One of the downsides of this tech is it will stifle new artistic styles that would have come about. How can any new artist hope to stand out. It's sort of like this trend to put older or deceased actors into new media but as they looked when they were young. It locks potential future roles to actors and actresses that existed in a certain point in time and says "We don't need new faces or different attempts at this character."  That said, the physical artist that documents their creative works will have their work much more valued. Anything released without a time lapse of creation will be suspect. Consider this: you can tell an AI "Greg Rutkowski", because Greg has been putting out art for decades. If AI puts artists out of business, we'll be forever frozen in this moment in time, where a lot of great art is around, but AIs won't add anything fresh to it. No new styles, just retreads. There's nothing wrong with AI being able to magically master (or apply)_ the style of Greg Rutkowski, as long as we can come up with a system where artists still get paid to create a body of work to ... I want to say 'inspire' AI. And perhaps we can get AI to keep track of what it used for inspiration, so that the original artist can be credited or even paid for their contribution (however small) to the derivative work. In the end though, brands, content creators, movie directors, etc. - they will want something truly fresh; but the market for mass producing art will quickly shrink, or rather grow and be mostly automated. So we primarily need to worry about how we're going to be nurturing and discovering the Greg Rutkowskis of the future.

I understand the fear a lot of people have when it comes to AI art, but as an artist with a lot of artist friends, as well as creative friends (writers/programmers) who have no artistic talent, I see it largely as a boon. As an artist it allows you to rapidly prototype and can even act as a muse, coming up with ideas you'd never think of on your own. As a creative, it gives you a way to realize your vision even if your hands lack the skill to do so, without having to spend potentially hundreds or thousands of dollars paying an artist out of pocket.  And while it's early days yet, it will allow an artist to dramatically increase the amount of work they can output. One of my friends and I have already been discussing putting together an indie RPG with hand-painted backgrounds for every scene, something which would take tens of thousands of dollars and hundreds of hours to do for a human artist, but which can be achieved by MidJourney in about 40 minutes.

As an artist myself, I think people who are concerned with "AI taking over my job in the art industry" aren't seeing the bigger picture. It is a valid concern for sure. However in my view, artists should be more focused on why they would need to work for a large studio in the first place once we get to that point. In a world where just a single person or small team can make a professional looking movie, game, tv show or whatever it may be... how would relying on a paycheck from a giant company make sense? The need for giant companies making art/content is going to go away pretty quickly when the playing field is leveled that much. Despite the future world where anyone is able to generate "high-fidelity content" still, the best idea wins. Artists are going to have all the tools in the world to create stuff of the likes we haven't seen before at a fraction of the cost and man-power. A well-made large scale piece of art like a film, still needs art direction from someone who knows their stuff. The end result is going to be better stuff being put out into world from the imagination of human beings, not big studios dependent on share holders. Stable Diffusion is free, open source and anyone can add to it with improvements (like Dreambooth in the video here). It has proven that the biggest developments from AI will come from online communities, not the big companies. I think this is going to be an independent revolution even bigger than YouTube itself and I'm excited.

While the generated imagery is incredible at first glance, and it is, professional artists shouldn't be too alarmed over this. 'Cause look closely, and you'll see cracks in every image, cracks that are a lot more serious and substantial than you may think. Solving issues like hands, tongues, eyes, meaningless chunks and "shadows," proportions of non-human bodies (and even human ones a lot of the time) and many others may take years and even decades, and there's no guarantee that they will ever be solved. The problem is that an AI by its nature cannot understand what it's creating, because the images are flat representations of 3D objects affected by physics. You can stuff as many pictures into the source database as you want, and it will never be enough, because flat images offer shortcuts to surface-level mimicry, not actual rules of how everything should look like with 100% real-world accuracy. On a rudimentary level, it's going to be awesome, it already is. But getting exactly what you want and without silly robot-esque mistakes won't be a certainty with machines for a very long time.  Case in point: automated translators. Google Translate has been around for 16 years. DeepL, a far more sophisticated deep-learning system, for 5 years. They still regularly make the dumbest mistakes because they are incapable of understanding what they're translating. They merely adapt known patterns. They're obviously incapable of properly translating jokes or passages meant to chill or evoke other emotions. You want a proper translation, you hire a translator with an editor. Same will remain true for artists for a very long while.

Despite how hard I try to remain optimistic, it‚Äôs pretty stressful watching AI progress as a young 3D artist. I quite honestly can‚Äôt imagine life being worth living for me if the visual art industry is automated. It‚Äôs difficult not to get emotional about it because I'm so passionate about my career and one day, all the work I put in/experience I have could mean essentially nothing. Over the last few months I've had to seriously consider a career shift. Maybe there isn't much of a future anymore in this industry to provide for a family. And is it fair to take that risk if it puts the future of my small family in financial jeopardy?   Additionally, artists are already constantly shamed for all their quirks but only accepted because their skills prove useful to many businesses who rely on their creative talent. I cant imagine how poorly artists will be treated by if their fine art skills served no monetary value. I've always been a bit different. Often, the only way I can freely express that and have others admire my humanity is through art.   If AI is to replace all labor, I hope it goes fast enough to automate most manual labor before it forces those with creative jobs into factory sweat shops. I‚Äôd rather be f&@king dead than work an boring, awful job for decades just because society hasn‚Äôt figured out how to automate it yet. You can tell me to suck it up. Touch√©. I just don't see the point in living life that way to be honest. The only thing that keeps me optimistic is that I love what I do so much I shouldn‚Äôt really care if a robot can do it better than me. I do it because it brings me peace and calms my mind. It‚Äôs no secret, art is therapy for many artists. However, I'd be lying if I said I haven't been struggling to find purpose now. For now, I'm just going to continue making whatever I desire and work to build a supportive community.  Handmade clothing and jewelry still exists despite much of the field being automated so that‚Äôs somewhat promising. So hopefully when all digital art can be automated, our society will find a way to value and support digital artists.    I‚Äôm always cautious to publicly comment on the topic because it‚Äôs so emotionally charged, I‚Äôm afraid I‚Äôll change my mind tomorrow. Nonetheless, this is how I often feel about it when my anxiety goes through the roof. It kind of feels like I watched my dream career fall apart in front of my eyes. I'm lost to say the least. Thank you for reading this far :)

As an artist, I think the tech is cool - but I do not think we should ever let AI generated images fall under any copyright. It wasn't created by anyone.  I do think it can be useful as a tool but I think there is where the line should be drawn. It does genuinely worry me that this will make it even harder for artists to actually make a living off of their art. The art industry is already massively undervalued both on the indie scale and still very much so on a corporate level. AI images may just worsen this problem, even though it wouldn't exist without all the artists making the works used to train the AI. I'm not sure yet how I feel about that, I don't think it's possible for something not sentient to be 'inspired', since it's just an algorithm meshing things together. The result may look pretty much the same though, and I think that's all most people will care about.  On a personal level, it's really scary to me. Because art is one of the only ways I have to make money due to medical and other issues, and it's something i love doing. I genuinely hope that as the tech becomes more widespread that these kinds of things get taken seriously.

This will definitely put a crimp in still art. I would not mind seeing my characters in some other artists style. But having to manually add addition or specific information so the A.I. does not go off the deep end with hundreds of variations and NONE of them are at all like what you are after. So, I don't see AIGA being too much of an issue beyond being yet another tool.  It may also help in some areas. The ever difficult "one-man-show", will certainly be helped if A.I. takes care of more time consuming parts that are better left to automation, like Texturing, HDRIs, Compositing, image asset gathering or generating, as advance rigging set-ups (such as muscle sim set ups), running driven parts of a scene or rig. among other things. Things that can be quantified mathematically like explosions, solid object physics , the weeble-wobble of soft body physics are already A.I. to some degree.  Actual acting in animation or Motion Capture (MoCap) will be humans for the foreseeable future. This especially if something ACTUALLY NEW needs to be done. Even then fine tuning will still need to be done, and that will remain human.   AIGA is not the end and I don't think it will be as far reaching as many fear it is. But it remains something to keep an eye on.

There is something sinister about this tech in that it feeds off human imagination. It cannot currently exist without it. Not unlike the way social media feeds off human attention. Be wary of letting it consume your thoughts and vivid imaginations (or artistic content for that matter) for free as one day it will not need that input to function.   Our attention, thoughts and imagination is where our greatest power (and value) lies. Big tech KNOWS this. Do not give these things freely to the machine. The lessons we have learned through social media tearing apart the fabric of society will serve us well when it comes to reigning in the AI beast system. The input to these systems is ALWAYS more valuable than the output, forget this at your peril.

People who used to make equipment hor horses and horse-pulled vehicles complained that the new-fangled "autovehicle" would endanger their jobs.  A few of them changed along with the times and re-focused their work to accommodate the changing roles of horse transport, but those who wouldn't or couldn't were pushed out.  Is it a good or bad thing that most of them ended up unable to work they way they always had?  Was it fair for them to be forced to re-specialize or learn a new job just to support themselves?  Should we have banned cars to save their jobs? Large farming and construction equipment like tractors greatly reduced the need for manual labor in these fields.  The need to dig or plant or harvest by hand is greatly diminished and work in these fields requires training to operate large work equipment.  Should we avoid them to allow more jobs to be had? Automated assembly in factories forced people working production lines to either adapt and learn to use the equipment, or abandon the field and find something else they can manage.  Should we go back to requiring everything to be made by hand? Technological innovation and progress *will* keep progressing.  Sure, there were times in the past when technological developments were suppressed.  In ancient Rome, for instance, they outlawed many labor-saving devices in the name of not crashing the slave trade that their various conquests supported and also to "protect public order" by avoiding putting large portions of the population out of work (unemployed people are desperate people).  Well, we all know what eventually happened to the Roman Empire. The *only* thing you can do in response to automation taking your job is to learn and adapt to *using* those systems to do what your job will change into.  And, eventually, our entire concept of economy will need to change when too few jobs require a person to do them that there's barely *any* work for people to do.  We need, and we need it fast, a way to accommodate people existing *without* the archaic, obsolete notion that a person *must* do some work to justify their livelihood.  We need to transition into a socioeconomic paradigm where having a job is an optional prospect; those who enjoy the work can do it at their own discretion, but it's also just as viable to not have a job.  If we don't, we aren't going to "keep the jobs"; those are going away one way or another no matter who likes it or not.  The only choice to make is how we *handle* not having enough "people work" to go around.

The issue with AI art is that it can only generate results. It cannot generate consistency. AI art works up to a point...and not a very far point either. Once you get into the nitty gritty process of reviews, model sheets, lighting design, and shot composition, you cannot depend on algorithmic dice rolls. People can create beautiful personal portfolios of work with this technology, but they are only cheating themselves in the long term. That portfolio might get them a job at a studio, but they'll be immediately sunk as soon as a director starts asking for specific intricacies...and hands.

The storyboarding applications of this are incredible
The storyboarding applications of this are incredible
What do you do beyond that? Lots of photoshoping and changing of small details and parts, like the hands... or adding small details to clothing, shapes, character, frills. In order to really make a specific piece you want you do need to work on and guide this in with a good bit of effort most of the time. But even then, the general design is still directly based off of someone elses work, but honestly a lot of my own paintings nearly copied or heavily references one or two other artists styles, so is it different when I copy it by hand versus using a machine to copy?  Ya, honestly no idea, as far as I'm concerned, it's an interesting tool but legally quite questionable. And since it can't do things that are "new" very well, it's also really limited in it's viability. Like I want to make a combat drone... but not enough artists or pictures of combat drones of the type I want exist for it to make them worth a damn, or what about using my own new design for a combat drone? I made my own model for a game, i want some cool art for it, the current AI can't figure that out as well yet, it can't come up with a new design of a new ship or character unless it can draw from existing works on individual elements and parts to create them. If no artists make stuff with sombreros, I can't have it make an elf wearing a sombrero. So we still rely on artists to make the art we can train these off of, they do basically just rehash existing art/patterns they've been trained on into new combinations, not really much more yet. So for now... they aren't replacing arists by any means, that said... another five, ten, or twenty years... I honestly wouldn't be surprised if I could type in or even talk and describe what I want and get real time updates on something, make comments on parts to change and literally design a new vehicle or space ship or tree village or something interactively and then have the AI save the design/idea of that for reference in future works. Really, this techs advancing super fast so it's hard to say where it will end up.

I love the dreambooth/textual inversion based story, but I'm sad to see that they risk propagating the misconception that the AI image generators "are like a library". This leads people to believe it goes out on the internet and find real artists images and mixes them together and outputs it. I've seen lots of magazines describing the process like this as well, which obviously has the artists complaining. The *training data* consists of public images on the internet, billions of them, but the end result is self-contained locally on the computer and is a gigabyte worth of neural network weights or so, that on a high level encapsulates all the art/photo knowledge available on the web, like a very very very skilled human artist that also have seen and knows about all the other artists and photographers and objects in the world.

ARTISTS: here's some things we can do to feel a little less doomed about all this: - Broaden your styles: absorb the works of all kinds of different artists across the world and history, study their styles and what makes them unique. Practice in styles outside your comfort zone. - Learn the 'defaults' of the AI: moody lighting, orangeblue color grading, flat camera angles, tons of rimlight... these are EVERYWHERE in AIart because they were also *everywhere* in the last decade of human art that it was trained on. - Show your process: Post WIPs, do timelapses, record/stream yourself, add that *humanity* to your art! Everyone will appreciate it.
